<td colspan="2" style="text-align: center; font-size: 88%">Range of the saltwater crocodile in black</td>

<p>The <b>saltwater crocodile</b> (<i>Crocodylus porosus</i>), also known as the <b>estuarine crocodile</b>, <b>Indo-Pacific crocodile</b>, <b>marine crocodile</b>, <b>sea crocodile</b> or informally as <b>saltie</b>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> is the largest of all living reptiles, as well as the largest <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Riparian" title="Riparian">riparian</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Predator" title="Predator">predator</a> in the world. Males of this species can reach sizes up to at least 6.30 m (20.7 ft) and possibly up to 7.0 m (23.0 ft) in length.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britton_et_al._2012_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-Britton_et_al._2012-3">[3]</a></sup> However, an adult male saltwater crocodile rarely reaches and exceeds a size of 6 m (19.7 ft) weighing 1,000 to 1,200 kg (2,200–2,600 lb).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ADW_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-ADW-4">[4]</a></sup> Females are much smaller and often do not surpass 3 m (9.8 ft).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ADW_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-ADW-4">[4]</a></sup> As its name implies, this species of <a href="/wiki/Crocodile" title="Crocodile">crocodile</a> can live in marine environments, but usually resides in saline and brackish <a href="/wiki/Mangrove_swamp" title="Mangrove swamp">mangrove swamps</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Estuaries" title="Estuaries">estuaries</a>, <a href="/wiki/River_delta" title="River delta">deltas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lagoon" title="Lagoon">lagoons</a>, and lower stretches of <a href="/wiki/River" title="River">rivers</a>. They have the broadest <a href="/wiki/Range_(biology)" title="Range (biology)">distribution</a> of any modern crocodile, ranging from the eastern coast of India, throughout most of Southeast Asia, and northern Australia.</p>
<p>The saltwater crocodile is a formidable and opportunistic <a href="/wiki/Hypercarnivore" title="Hypercarnivore">hypercarnivorous</a> <a href="/wiki/Apex_predator" title="Apex predator">apex predator</a>. Most prey are <a href="/wiki/Ambush_predator" title="Ambush predator">ambushed</a> and then drowned or swallowed whole. It is capable of prevailing over almost any animal that enters its territory, including other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apex_predators" title="Apex predators">apex predators</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Shark" title="Shark">sharks</a>, varieties of <a href="/wiki/Freshwater_fish" title="Freshwater fish">freshwater</a> and <a href="/wiki/Saltwater_fish" title="Saltwater fish">marine fish</a> including <a href="/wiki/Pelagic_fish" title="Pelagic fish">pelagic</a> species, <a href="/wiki/Invertebrate" title="Invertebrate">invertebrates</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Crustacean" title="Crustacean">crustaceans</a>, various <a href="/wiki/Reptile" title="Reptile">reptiles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bird" title="Bird">birds</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Crocodile_attack" title="Crocodile attack">humans</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Callaway.2C_J._M._1997_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Callaway.2C_J._M._1997-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blaber.2C_S._J._2008_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-Blaber.2C_S._J._2008-6">[6]</a></sup> Due to their size, aggression and distribution, saltwater crocodiles are regarded as the most dangerous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extant_taxon" title="Extant taxon">extant</a> <a href="/wiki/Crocodilia" title="Crocodilia">crocodilian</a> to humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ross_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ross-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-18"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>

</div>
<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Taxonomy_and_evolution">Taxonomy and evolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Saltwater_crocodile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Taxonomy and evolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><i>Crocodylus porosus</i> is believed to have a direct link to similar crocodilians that inhabited the shorelines of the supercontinent <a href="/wiki/Gondwana" title="Gondwana">Gondwana</a> (which included what is now the Australian continent) as long ago as 98 million years and were survivors of the <a href="/wiki/Cretaceous%E2%80%93Paleogene_extinction_event" title="Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event">Cretaceous–Paleogene extinction event</a>. Fossils of <a href="/wiki/Isisfordia" title="Isisfordia">Isisfordia</a>, discovered in outback western <a href="/wiki/Queensland" title="Queensland">Queensland</a> (once a vast inland sea) though smaller in size, show attributes of direct lineage to <i>Crocodylus porosus</i>, suggesting it occupied a similar habitat, with vertebrae indicating it shared the ability to death roll during feeding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> Incomplete fossil records make it difficult to accurately trace the emergence of the species. The genome was fully sequenced in 2007.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> The earliest fossil evidence of the species dates to around 4.0–4.5 million years ago <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> and no subspecies are known. Scientists estimate that <i>C. porosus</i> is an ancient species that could have diverged from 12 to 6 million years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oaks_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-oaks-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> Genetic research has unsurprisingly indicated that the saltwater crocodile is related relatively closely to other living species of Asian crocodile, although some ambiguity exists over what assemblage it could be considered part of based on variable genetic results. Other relatively broad-snouted species such as <a href="/wiki/Mugger_crocodile" title="Mugger crocodile">Mugger</a> (<i>C. palustris</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Siamese_crocodile" title="Siamese crocodile">Siamese crocodiles</a> (<i>C. siamensis</i>) seem to be the most likely candidates to bear the closest relation among living species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oaks_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-oaks-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Naish_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Naish-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Possible_subspecies_and_status_as_a_species_complex">Possible subspecies and status as a species complex</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Saltwater_crocodile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Possible subspecies and status as a species complex">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Currently, most sources state that the saltwater crocodile does not have a subspecies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brazaitis_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brazaitis-20">[20]</a></sup> However, based largely on morphological variability, some have claimed that not only are there subspecies but that <i>C. porosus</i> actually houses a species complex. In 1844, S. Müller and H. Schlegel attempted to describe crocodiles from <a href="/wiki/Java" title="Java">Java</a> and <a href="/wiki/Borneo" title="Borneo">Borneo</a> as a new species which they named <i>C. raninus</i>, subsequently given the informal common names of the Indonesian crocodile or Bornean crocodile. According to Ross (1992), specimens of <i>C. raninus</i> can reliably be distinguished both from Siamese crocodiles and true saltwater crocodiles on the basis of the number ventral scales and on the presence of four postoccipital scutes which are often absent in true saltwater crocodiles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Naish_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-Naish-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Another attempt to derive a species came from Australia, Wells &amp; Wellington (1985), and was based upon large-bodied, relatively large-headed and short-tailed crocodiles from Australia. The type specimen reported for this so-called species was a crocodile nicknamed "Sweetheart" that was inadvertently killed in 1979 (drowned after overly-anesthetized in an attempt to relocate it after it had taken to attacking boats). However, this "species", <i>C. pethericki</i>, has later been largely considered as a misinterpretation of the physiological changes undergone by very large male crocodiles. However, Wells and Wellington's assertion that the Australian saltwater crocodiles may at least be distinctive enough from northern Asian saltwater crocodiles to warrant subspecies status, as could <i>raninus</i> from other Asian saltwater crocodiles, has been considered to possibly bear validity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Naish_19-2"><a href="#cite_note-Naish-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">Characteristics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Saltwater_crocodile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Characteristics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Saltieskull.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2448" data-file-width="3264" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/fd/Saltieskull.JPG/220px-Saltieskull.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/fd/Saltieskull.JPG/330px-Saltieskull.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/fd/Saltieskull.JPG/440px-Saltieskull.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Saltieskull.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Saltwater crocodile skull from <a href="/wiki/Zoological_Museum_of_the_Zoological_Institute_of_the_Russian_Academy_of_Sciences" title="Zoological Museum of the Zoological Institute of the Russian Academy of Sciences">The Museum of Zoology</a>, Russia. Note the considerably more slender skull of a <a href="/wiki/Gharial" title="Gharial">gharial</a> in the background.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Crocodile_Crocodylus-porosus_amk2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2035" data-file-width="3053" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Crocodile_Crocodylus-porosus_amk2.jpg/220px-Crocodile_Crocodylus-porosus_amk2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Crocodile_Crocodylus-porosus_amk2.jpg/330px-Crocodile_Crocodylus-porosus_amk2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Crocodile_Crocodylus-porosus_amk2.jpg/440px-Crocodile_Crocodylus-porosus_amk2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Crocodile_Crocodylus-porosus_amk2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Head of a saltwater crocodile</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The saltwater crocodile has a wide snout compared to most crocodiles. However, it has a longer <a href="/wiki/Snout" title="Snout">muzzle</a> than the <a href="/wiki/Mugger_crocodile" title="Mugger crocodile">mugger crocodile</a>; its length is twice its width at the base.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guggisberg_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Guggisberg-26">[26]</a></sup> The saltwater crocodile has fewer armour plates on its neck than other crocodilians. On this species, a pair of ridges runs from the eyes along the centre of the snout. The scales are oval in shape and the <a href="/wiki/Scute" title="Scute">scutes</a> are either small compared to other species or commonly are entirely absent. In addition, an obvious gap is also present between the cervical and dorsal shields, and small, triangular scutes are present between the posterior edges of the large, transversely arranged scutes in the dorsal shield. The relative lack of scutes is considered an asset useful to distinguish saltwater crocodiles in captivity or in illicit leather trading, as well as in the few areas in the field where sub-adult or younger saltwater crocodiles may need to be distinguished from other crocodiles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> The adult saltwater crocodile's broad body contrasts with that of most other lean crocodiles, leading to early unverified assumptions the reptile was an <a href="/wiki/Alligator" title="Alligator">alligator</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ufl_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-ufl-29">[29]</a></sup> The head is very large. The largest skull sized that could be scientifically verified was for a specimen in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mus%C3%A9um_national_d%27Histoire_naturelle" title="Muséum national d'Histoire naturelle">Muséum national d'Histoire naturelle</a> sourced to <a href="/wiki/Cambodia" title="Cambodia">Cambodia</a>, the skull length for this specimen was 76 cm (30 in) (female skull lengths of over 45 cm (18 in) are exceptional), with a mandibular length of 98.3 cm (38.7 in) and a maximum width across the skull (near the base) of 48 cm (19 in). The length of the specimen this came from is not known but based on skull-to-total-length ratios for very large saltwater crocodiles its length was presumably somewhere in the 7 m (23 ft 0 in) range.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Whitaker_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Whitaker-32">[32]</a></sup> Although it is the largest overall living crocodilian and reptile, other crocodilians may have a proportionately longer skull, namely the <a href="/wiki/Gharial" title="Gharial">gharial</a> (<i>Gavialis gangeticus</i>) and the <a href="/wiki/False_gharial" title="False gharial">false gharial</a> (<i>Tomistoma schlegelii</i>), skull lengths in the latter have been verified up to 84 cm (33 in) (the largest crocodilian skull verified for a living species), although both of these thin-snouted species have less massive skulls and considerably less massive bodies than the saltwater crocodile.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Whitaker_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-Whitaker-32">[32]</a></sup> The teeth are also long, with the largest teeth (the fourth tooth from the front on the lower jaw) having been measured at up to 9 cm (3.5 in) in length.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> If detached from the body, the head of a very large male crocodile can reportedly weigh over 200 kg (440 lb) alone, including the large muscles and tendons at the base of the skull that lend the crocodile its massive biting strength.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wood_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wood-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p>Young saltwater crocodiles are pale yellow in colour with black stripes and spots on their bodies and tails. This colouration lasts for several years until the crocodiles mature into adults. The colour as an adult is much darker greenish-drab, with a few lighter tan or grey areas sometimes apparent. Several colour variations are known and some adults may retain fairly pale skin, whereas others may be so dark as to appear blackish. The ventral surface is white or yellow in colour on saltwater crocodiles of all ages. Stripes are present on the lower sides of their bodies, but do not extend onto their bellies. Their tails are grey with dark bands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britton_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Britton-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Size">Size</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Saltwater_crocodile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Size">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MAGNT_Sweetheart.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="3008" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/MAGNT_Sweetheart.JPG/220px-MAGNT_Sweetheart.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/MAGNT_Sweetheart.JPG/330px-MAGNT_Sweetheart.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/MAGNT_Sweetheart.JPG/440px-MAGNT_Sweetheart.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MAGNT_Sweetheart.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Sweetheart_(crocodile)" title="Sweetheart (crocodile)">Sweetheart</a>, a large crocodile, at about 5.1 m (16 ft 9 in) and scaled to weigh 816 kg (1,799 lb). At one point, Sweetheart led some authors to consider a separate Australian species of saltwater crocodile (<i>C. pethericki</i>).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Saltwater crocodiles are the largest extant riparian predators in the world. However, they start life fairly small. Newly hatched saltwater crocodiles measure about 28 cm (11 in) long and weigh an average of 71 g (2.5 oz).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NewHolland_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-NewHolland-38">[38]</a></sup> This distinct contrast in size between hatchlings and adult males is one of the greatest in terrestrial vertebrates.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> By their second year, young crocodiles grow to 1 m (3 ft 3 in) long and weigh 2.5 kg (5.5 lb).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NewHolland_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-NewHolland-38">[38]</a></sup> Males reach sexual maturity around 3.3 m (10 ft 10 in) at around 16 years of age, while females reach sexual maturity at 2.1 m (6 ft 11 in) and 12–14 years of age.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britton_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-Britton-37">[37]</a></sup> The weight of a crocodile increases exponentially as length increases.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[note 1]</a></sup> This explains why individuals at 6 m (19 ft 8 in) can weigh more than twice that of individuals at 5 m (16 ft).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ufl_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-ufl-29">[29]</a></sup> In crocodiles, linear growth eventually decreases and they start getting bulkier at a certain point.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[39]</a></sup> Dominant males also tend to outweigh others, as they maintain prime <a href="/wiki/Territory_(animal)" title="Territory (animal)">territories</a> with access to better, more abundant <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prey" title="Prey">prey</a>.</p>
<p><b>Male size</b>: An adult male saltwater crocodile, from young adults to older individuals, ranges 3.5 to 6 m (11 ft 6 in to 19 ft 8 in) in length, weighing 200 to 1,000 kg (440–2,200 lb).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Webb_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Webb-42">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Webb2_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Webb2-43">[42]</a></sup> On average, adult males range 4.3 to 4.9 m (14 ft 1 in to 16 ft 1 in) in length and weigh 408 to 522 kg (899–1,151 lb).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wood_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wood-35">[35]</a></sup> However average size largely depends on the location, habitat, and human interactions, thus changes from one study to another, when figures of each study are viewed separately. In one case, Webb and Manolis (1989) attributed the average weight of adult males in Australian tidal rivers as only 240 to 350 kg (530 to 770 lb) at lengths of 4 to 4.5 m (13 ft 1 in to 14 ft 9 in) during the 1980s, possibly representing a reduced body mass due to the species being in recovery after decades of overhunting at that stage, as males this size would typically weigh about 100 kg (220 lb) heavier.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Webb_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-Webb-42">[41]</a></sup> Rarely very large, aged males can exceed 6 m (19 ft 8 in) in length and weigh well over 1,000 kg (2,200 lb).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ufl_29-2"><a href="#cite_note-ufl-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Whitaker_32-2"><a href="#cite_note-Whitaker-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[43]</a></sup> The largest confirmed saltwater crocodile on record drowned in a fishing net in <a href="/wiki/Papua_New_Guinea" title="Papua New Guinea">Papua New Guinea</a> in 1979, its dried skin plus head measured 6.2 m (20 ft 4 in) long and it was estimated to have been 6.3 m (20 ft 8 in) when accounting for shrinkage and a missing tail tip.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britton_et_al._2012_3-1"><a href="#cite_note-Britton_et_al._2012-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Whitaker.26Whitaker2008_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Whitaker.26Whitaker2008-45">[44]</a></sup> However, according to evidence, in the form of skulls coming from some of the largest crocodiles ever shot, the maximum possible size attained by the largest members of this species is considered to be 7 m (23 ft 0 in).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britton_et_al._2012_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-Britton_et_al._2012-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ufl_29-3"><a href="#cite_note-ufl-29">[29]</a></sup> A research paper on the morphology and physiology of crocodilians by the government of Australia estimates that saltwater crocodiles approaching sizes of 7 m (23 ft 0 in) would weigh around 2,000 kg (4,400 lb).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EnvironmentAU_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-EnvironmentAU-46">[45]</a></sup> Due to extensive <a href="/wiki/Poaching" title="Poaching">poaching</a> during the 20th century, such individuals are extremely rare today in most areas, as it takes a long time for the crocodiles to attain those sizes. Also, a possible earlier presence of particular <a href="/wiki/Gene" title="Gene">genes</a> may have led to such large-sized saltwater crocodiles, genes that were ultimately lost from the overall <a href="/wiki/Gene_pool" title="Gene pool">gene pool</a> due to extensive hide and trophy hunting in the past.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> However, with recent restoration of saltwater crocodile habitat and reduced <a href="/wiki/Poaching" title="Poaching">poaching</a>, the number of large crocodiles is increasing, especially in <a href="/wiki/Bhitarkanika_National_Park" title="Bhitarkanika National Park">Odisha</a>. This species is the only extant crocodilian to regularly reach or exceed 5.2 m (17 ft 1 in).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Whitaker_32-3"><a href="#cite_note-Whitaker-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wood_35-2"><a href="#cite_note-Wood-35">[35]</a></sup> A large male from Philippines, named <a href="/wiki/Lolong" title="Lolong">Lolong</a>, was the largest saltwater crocodile ever caught and placed in captivity. He was 20 ft 3 in (6.17 m), and weighed 2,370 lbs (1,075 kg). Believed to have eaten two villagers, Lolong was captured on 3 September 2011, and died in captivity on 10 February 2013.</p>
<p><b>Female size</b>: Adult females typically measure from 2.7 to 3.1 m (8 ft 10 in to 10 ft 2 in) in total length and weigh 76 to 103 kg (168 to 227 lb).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Campbell_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Campbell-48">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[48]</a></sup> The largest female on record measured about 4.2 m (13 ft 9 in) in total length.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wood_35-3"><a href="#cite_note-Wood-35">[35]</a></sup> Due to the extreme sexual dimorphism of the species as contrasted with the more modest size dimorphism of other species, the average length of the species is only slightly more than some other extant crocodilians at 3.8–4 m (12 ft 6 in–13 ft 1 in).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brazaitis_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-Brazaitis-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guggisberg_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-Guggisberg-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[49]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Distribution_and_habitat">Distribution and habitat</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Saltwater_crocodile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Distribution and habitat">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Leistenkrokodil.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="575" data-file-width="400" height="244" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Leistenkrokodil.jpg/170px-Leistenkrokodil.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Leistenkrokodil.jpg/255px-Leistenkrokodil.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Leistenkrokodil.jpg/340px-Leistenkrokodil.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>

</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">

<div class="thumb tright">

</div>
</div>
<p>The saltwater crocodile is one of the three crocodilians found in India, the other two being the more regionally widespread, smaller <a href="/wiki/Mugger_crocodile" title="Mugger crocodile">mugger crocodile</a> and the narrow-snouted, fish-eating <a href="/wiki/Gharial" title="Gharial">gharial</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[50]</a></sup> Apart from the eastern coast of India, the saltwater crocodile is extremely rare on the Indian subcontinent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[51]</a></sup> A large population is present within the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bhitarkanika_Wildlife_Sanctuary" title="Bhitarkanika Wildlife Sanctuary">Bhitarkanika Wildlife Sanctuary</a> of Odisha and they are known to be present in smaller numbers throughout the Indian and Bangladeshi portions of the <a href="/wiki/Sundarbans" title="Sundarbans">Sundarbans</a>. The saltwater crocodile also persists in bordering <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh" title="Bangladesh">Bangladesh</a> as does the mugger and gharial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[53]</a></sup> Populations are also present within the mangrove forests and other coastal areas of the <a href="/wiki/Andaman_and_Nicobar_Islands" title="Andaman and Nicobar Islands">Andaman and Nicobar Islands</a> in India. Saltwater crocodiles were once present throughout most of the island of <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">Sri Lanka</a>, but remain mostly within protected areas such as <a href="/wiki/Yala_National_Park" title="Yala National Park">Yala National Park</a>, which also has a large population of mugger crocodiles.</p>
<p>In northern Australia (which includes the northernmost parts of the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Territory" title="Northern Territory">Northern Territory</a>, Western Australia, and <a href="/wiki/Queensland" title="Queensland">Queensland</a>), the saltwater crocodile is thriving, particularly in the multiple river systems near <a href="/wiki/Darwin,_Northern_Territory" title="Darwin, Northern Territory">Darwin</a> such as the Adelaide, Mary, and Daly Rivers, along with their adjacent <a href="/wiki/Billabong" title="Billabong">billabongs</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Estuaries" title="Estuaries">estuaries</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[54]</a></sup> The saltwater crocodile population in Australia is estimated at 100,000 to 200,000 adults. In Australia, the species coexists with the smaller, narrow-snouted <a href="/wiki/Freshwater_crocodile" title="Freshwater crocodile">Johnston's or freshwater crocodile</a> (<i>C. johnstoni</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[55]</a></sup> Their range extends from <a href="/wiki/Broome,_Western_Australia" title="Broome, Western Australia">Broome</a> in Western Australia through the entire Northern Territory coast all the way south to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rockhampton,_Queensland" title="Rockhampton, Queensland">Rockhampton</a> in Queensland. The <a href="/wiki/Alligator_Rivers" title="Alligator Rivers">Alligator Rivers</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Arnhem_Land" title="Arnhem Land">Arnhem Land</a> region are misnamed due to the resemblance of the saltwater crocodile to alligators as compared to <a href="/wiki/Freshwater_crocodile" title="Freshwater crocodile">freshwater crocodiles</a>, which also inhabit the Northern Territory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[56]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/New_Guinea" title="New Guinea">New Guinea</a>, they are also common, existing within the coastal reaches of virtually every river system in the country, such as the <a href="/wiki/Fly_River" title="Fly River">Fly River</a>, along with all estuaries and mangroves, where they overlap in range but rarely in actuality or habitat with the rarer, less aggressive <a href="/wiki/New_Guinea_crocodile" title="New Guinea crocodile">New Guinea crocodile</a> (<i>C. novaeguineae</i>). They are also present in varying numbers throughout the <a href="/wiki/Bismarck_Archipelago" title="Bismarck Archipelago">Bismarck Archipelago</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Kai_Islands" title="Kai Islands">Kai Islands</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Aru_Islands" title="Aru Islands">Aru Islands</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Maluku_Islands" title="Maluku Islands">Maluku Islands</a> and many other islands within the region, including <a href="/wiki/Timor" title="Timor">Timor</a>, and most islands within the <a href="/wiki/Torres_Strait" title="Torres Strait">Torres Strait</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[57]</a></sup></p>
<p>The saltwater crocodile was historically known to be widespread throughout Southeast Asia, but is now extinct throughout much of this range. This species has not been reported in the wild for decades in most of <a href="/wiki/Indochina" title="Indochina">Indochina</a> and is extinct in <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[58]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Laos" title="Laos">Laos</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Whitaker2_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Whitaker2-60">[59]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[60]</a></sup> and possibly <a href="/wiki/Cambodia" title="Cambodia">Cambodia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[61]</a></sup> The status of this species is critical within much of <a href="/wiki/Myanmar" title="Myanmar">Myanmar</a>, but a stable population of many large adults is present in the <a href="/wiki/Irrawaddy_Delta" title="Irrawaddy Delta">Irrawaddy Delta</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[62]</a></sup> Probably, the only country in Indochina still harbouring wild populations of this species is Myanmar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[63]</a></sup> Although saltwater crocodiles were once very common in the <a href="/wiki/Mekong_Delta" title="Mekong Delta">Mekong Delta</a> (from where they disappeared in the 1980s) and other river systems, the future of this species in <a href="/wiki/Indochina" title="Indochina">Indochina</a> is now looking grim. However, it is also the least likely of crocodilians to become globally extinct due to its wide distribution and almost precolonial population sizes in Northern Australia and <a href="/wiki/New_Guinea" title="New Guinea">New Guinea</a>.</p>
<p>The saltwater crocodile has been long extinct in China, where it inhabited the southern coastal areas from <a href="/wiki/Fujian" title="Fujian">Fujian</a> province in the north to the border of Vietnam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[64]</a></sup> References to large crocodiles that preyed on both humans and livestock appeared during the Han and Song Dynasties, where it occurred in the lower <a href="/wiki/Pearl_River_(China)" title="Pearl River (China)">Pearl River</a> near present-day Hong Kong and <a href="/wiki/Macau" title="Macau">Macau</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Han_River_(Guangdong)" title="Han River (Guangdong)">Han River</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Min_River_(Fujian)" title="Min River (Fujian)">Min River</a> in the north, portions of coastal <a href="/wiki/Guangxi" title="Guangxi">Guangxi</a> province and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hainan_Island" title="Hainan Island">Hainan Island</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guggisberg_26-2"><a href="#cite_note-Guggisberg-26">[26]</a></sup> The presence of crocodiles in Fujian province represent the northernmost distribution of the species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[65]</a></sup></p>
<p>The population is sporadic in <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a> the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a> and <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">Malaysia</a>, with some areas harbouring large populations (<a href="/wiki/Borneo" title="Borneo">Borneo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sumatra" title="Sumatra">Sumatra</a>, for example) and others with very small, at-risk populations (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Peninsular_Malaysia" title="Peninsular Malaysia">Peninsular Malaysia</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stuebing.2C_R._B._1994_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stuebing.2C_R._B._1994-67">[66]</a></sup> Despite the close proximity to the crocodile hotbed of northern Australia, crocodiles no longer exist in <a href="/wiki/Bali" title="Bali">Bali</a>. This species is also reportedly extinct on <a href="/wiki/Lombok" title="Lombok">Lombok</a>, <a href="/wiki/Komodo_(island)" title="Komodo (island)">Komodo</a>, and most of <a href="/wiki/Java" title="Java">Java</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[67]</a></sup> In the southern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Malaysian_Peninsula" title="Malaysian Peninsula">Malaysian Peninsula</a> as well as <a href="/wiki/Borneo" title="Borneo">Borneo</a>, salwater crocodiles may co-exist with the relatively narrow-snouted <a href="/wiki/False_gharial" title="False gharial">false gharial</a> (as well as on <a href="/wiki/Sumatra" title="Sumatra">Sumatra</a>) and the closely related but usually smaller <a href="/wiki/Siamese_crocodile" title="Siamese crocodile">Siamese crocodile</a> (as well as in <a href="/wiki/Java" title="Java">Java</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[69]</a></sup> A small population may remain within <a href="/wiki/Ujung_Kulon_National_Park" title="Ujung Kulon National Park">Ujung Kulon National Park</a> in western Java. The saltwater crocodile is also present in very limited parts of the South Pacific, with an average population in the <a href="/wiki/Solomon_Islands" title="Solomon Islands">Solomon Islands</a>, a very small and soon to be extinct population in <a href="/wiki/Vanuatu" title="Vanuatu">Vanuatu</a> (where the population officially stands at only three) and a decent but at-risk population (which may be rebounding) in <a href="/wiki/Palau" title="Palau">Palau</a>. They once ranged as far west as the east coast of Africa to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seychelles_Islands" title="Seychelles Islands">Seychelles Islands</a>. These crocodiles were once believed to be a population of <a href="/wiki/Nile_crocodile" title="Nile crocodile">Nile crocodiles</a>, but they were later proven to be <i>C. porosus</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ufl_29-4"><a href="#cite_note-ufl-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Saltwater_crocodile_on_a_beach_in_Darwin,_NT.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="700" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Saltwater_crocodile_on_a_beach_in_Darwin%2C_NT.jpg/220px-Saltwater_crocodile_on_a_beach_in_Darwin%2C_NT.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Saltwater_crocodile_on_a_beach_in_Darwin%2C_NT.jpg/330px-Saltwater_crocodile_on_a_beach_in_Darwin%2C_NT.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Saltwater_crocodile_on_a_beach_in_Darwin%2C_NT.jpg/440px-Saltwater_crocodile_on_a_beach_in_Darwin%2C_NT.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Saltwater_crocodile_on_a_beach_in_Darwin,_NT.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
On a beach in <a href="/wiki/Darwin,_Northern_Territory" title="Darwin, Northern Territory">Darwin</a>, Australia</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The saltwater crocodile has been extirpated from much of its former range within the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a> and currently populations remain in only a few scattered locations. On the island of <a href="/wiki/Mindanao" title="Mindanao">Mindanao</a> the species is present within the Ligawasan Marsh and adjacent waterways in the southwest of the island and also within the Agusan Marsh Wildlife Sanctuary in the eastern portion of the island, where in September of 2011 a 20.24 foot/6.17 meter saltwater crocodile, the largest reliably measured, was captured by locals following two fatal attacks. This species is also reported to be found within the mangroves of Languyan Island of the Tawi-Tawi province southwest of Mindanao and within the mangroves of Del Carmen municipality on Siargao Island northeast of Mindanao. On Luzon Island the species has been reported from three areas in the northeast: the Blos River of barangay Reina Mercedes, the Divilacan mangroves of barangay Dimasalansan and the Palanan River estuary of barangay Culasi. On the island of Palawan the species appears to only remain within the southern portion of the country and in recent years attacks on humans have occurred within the Rio Tuba area near Bataraza on the southeast coast and from near Rizal on the southeastern coast. The species is also reported to be present within the islands of the Balabac Strait between the island of Palawan and the Malaysian Borneo state of Sabah. The status of the species within Naujan Lake on the island of Mindoro is unclear; crocodile eye-shines have been spotted in recent years and these are believed to belong to the saltwater crocodile, but it is unknown if a viable population remains.</p>
<p>Because of its tendency to travel very long distances at sea, individual saltwater crocodiles have been known to occasionally appear in areas far away from their general range. Vagrant individuals have historically been reported on <a href="/wiki/New_Caledonia" title="New Caledonia">New Caledonia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fiji" title="Fiji">Fiji</a>, and in Asian waters possibly swam with <a href="/wiki/Kuroshio_Current" title="Kuroshio Current">Kuroshio Current</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ecolumn_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-ecolumn-71">[70]</a></sup> reaching such as at <a href="/wiki/Iwo_Jima" title="Iwo Jima">Iwo Jima</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hachij%C5%8D-jima" title="Hachijō-jima">Hachijō-jima</a>, <a href="/wiki/Amami_%C5%8Cshima" title="Amami Ōshima">Amami Ōshima</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iriomote-jima" title="Iriomote-jima">Iriomote-jima</a> (residences by several individuals along <a href="/wiki/Urauchi_River" title="Urauchi River">Urauchi River</a> from <a href="/wiki/Bakumatsu" title="Bakumatsu">Bakumatsu</a> to <a href="/wiki/Meiji_period" title="Meiji period">Meiji</a> until being hunted by locals were suggested)<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="I suspect poor translation from the Japanese. (October 2016)">further explanation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[71]</a></sup> pelagic waters off <a href="/wiki/Shima,_Mie" title="Shima, Mie">Shima, Mie</a>, <a href="/wiki/Miura_Peninsula" title="Miura Peninsula">Miura Peninsula</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ecolumn_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-ecolumn-71">[70]</a></sup> and even in the relatively frigid <a href="/wiki/Sea_of_Japan" title="Sea of Japan">Sea of Japan</a> (thousands of miles from their native territory.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kumar_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kumar-73">[72]</a></sup> In late 2008-early 2009, a handful of wild saltwater crocodiles were verified to be living within the river systems of <a href="/wiki/Fraser_Island" title="Fraser Island">Fraser Island</a>, hundreds of kilometres from, and in much cooler water than, their normal Queensland range. These crocodiles did indeed migrate south to the island from northern Queensland during the warmer <a href="/wiki/Wet_season" title="Wet season">wet season</a> and presumably returned to the north upon the seasonal temperature drop. Despite the surprise and shock within the Fraser Island public, this is apparently not new behaviour, and in the distant past, wild crocodiles had been reported occasionally appearing as far south as Brisbane during the warmer wet season.</p>
<p>Saltwater crocodiles generally spend the tropical wet season in freshwater swamps and rivers, moving downstream to estuaries in the dry season, and sometimes travelling far out to sea. Crocodiles compete fiercely with each other for territory, with dominant males in particular occupying the most eligible stretches of freshwater creeks and streams. Junior crocodiles are thus forced into the more marginal river systems and sometimes into the ocean. This explains the large distribution of the animal (ranging from the east coast of India to northern Australia), as well as its being found in the odd places on occasion (such as the Sea of Japan). Like all crocodiles, they can survive for prolonged periods only in warm temperatures, and crocodiles seasonally vacate parts of Australia if cold spells hit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ross_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ross-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biology_and_behaviour">Biology and behaviour</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Saltwater_crocodile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Biology and behaviour">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Crocodylus_porosus_foraging_in_surf_at_Maconacon_-_ZooKeys-266-001-g104-bottom.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1099" data-file-width="1512" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Crocodylus_porosus_foraging_in_surf_at_Maconacon_-_ZooKeys-266-001-g104-bottom.jpg/220px-Crocodylus_porosus_foraging_in_surf_at_Maconacon_-_ZooKeys-266-001-g104-bottom.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Crocodylus_porosus_foraging_in_surf_at_Maconacon_-_ZooKeys-266-001-g104-bottom.jpg/330px-Crocodylus_porosus_foraging_in_surf_at_Maconacon_-_ZooKeys-266-001-g104-bottom.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Crocodylus_porosus_foraging_in_surf_at_Maconacon_-_ZooKeys-266-001-g104-bottom.jpg/440px-Crocodylus_porosus_foraging_in_surf_at_Maconacon_-_ZooKeys-266-001-g104-bottom.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Crocodylus_porosus_foraging_in_surf_at_Maconacon_-_ZooKeys-266-001-g104-bottom.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A saltwater crocodile off <a href="/wiki/Maconacon,_Isabela" title="Maconacon, Isabela">Maconacon</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The primary behaviour to distinguish the saltwater crocodile from other <a href="/wiki/Crocodile" title="Crocodile">crocodiles</a> is its tendency to occupy salt water. Though other crocodiles also have <a href="/wiki/Salt_gland" title="Salt gland">salt glands</a> that enable them to survive in saltwater, a trait which alligators do not possess, most other species do not venture out to sea except during extreme conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[73]</a></sup> The only other species to display regular seagoing behaviour is the <a href="/wiki/American_crocodile" title="American crocodile">American crocodile</a> (<i>C. acutus</i>), but the American version is still not considered to be as marine-prone as the saltwater crocodile. As its alternate name "sea-going crocodile" implies, this species travels between areas separated by sea, or simply relies on the relative ease of travelling through water in order to circumvent long distances on the same land mass, such as Australia. In a similar fashion to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Migratory_bird" title="Migratory bird">migratory birds</a> using <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thermal_column" title="Thermal column">thermal columns</a>, saltwater crocodiles use <a href="/wiki/Ocean_current" title="Ocean current">ocean currents</a> to travel long distances.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc.co.uk_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-bbc.co.uk-75">[74]</a></sup> In a study, 20 crocodiles were tagged with <a href="/wiki/Animal_migration_tracking" title="Animal migration tracking">satellite transmitters</a>; 8 of these crocodiles ventured out into <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Open_ocean" title="Open ocean">open ocean</a>, in which one of them travelled 590 km (370 mi) along the coast – from the North Kennedy River on eastern coast of <a href="/wiki/Far_North_Queensland" title="Far North Queensland">Far North Queensland</a>, around <a href="/wiki/Cape_York_Peninsula" title="Cape York Peninsula">Cape York Peninsula</a>, to the west coast in the <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Carpentaria" title="Gulf of Carpentaria">Gulf of Carpentaria</a> – in 25 days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc.co.uk_75-1"><a href="#cite_note-bbc.co.uk-75">[74]</a></sup> Another specimen, a 4.84 m (15 ft 11 in)-long male, travelled 411 km (255 mi) in 20 days. Without having to move around much, sometimes simply by floating, the current-riding behaviour allows for the conservation of energy. They will even interrupt their travels, residing in sheltered bays for a few days, when the current is against the desired direction of travel, until the current changes direction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc.co.uk_75-2"><a href="#cite_note-bbc.co.uk-75">[74]</a></sup> Crocodiles also travel up and down in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/River_system" title="River system">river systems</a>, periodically.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bbc.co.uk_75-3"><a href="#cite_note-bbc.co.uk-75">[74]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Saltwater_Crocodile_(Crocodylus_porosus)_(8851850268).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1944" data-file-width="2592" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Saltwater_Crocodile_%28Crocodylus_porosus%29_%288851850268%29.jpg/220px-Saltwater_Crocodile_%28Crocodylus_porosus%29_%288851850268%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Saltwater_Crocodile_%28Crocodylus_porosus%29_%288851850268%29.jpg/330px-Saltwater_Crocodile_%28Crocodylus_porosus%29_%288851850268%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Saltwater_Crocodile_%28Crocodylus_porosus%29_%288851850268%29.jpg/440px-Saltwater_Crocodile_%28Crocodylus_porosus%29_%288851850268%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Saltwater_Crocodile_(Crocodylus_porosus)_(8851850268).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An adult saltwater crocodile swimming through a swamp.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>While most crocodilians are social animals sharing basking spots and food, saltwater crocodiles are more <a href="/wiki/Territory_(animal)" title="Territory (animal)">territorial</a> and are less tolerant of their own kind; adult males will share territory with females, but drive off rival males. Saltwater crocodiles mate in the wet season, laying <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Egg_(biology)" title="Egg (biology)">eggs</a> in a nest consisting of a mound of mud and vegetation. The female guards the <a href="/wiki/Nest" title="Nest">nest</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hatchling" title="Hatchling">hatchlings</a> from predators.</p>
<p>Generally very <a href="/wiki/Lethargy" title="Lethargy">lethargic</a>, a trait which helps it survive months at a time without food, the saltwater crocodile will usually loiter in the water or bask in the sun during much of the day, preferring to hunt at night. A study of seasonal saltwater crocodile behaviour in Australia indicated that they are more active and more likely to spend time in the water during the Australian summer; conversely, they are less active and spend relatively more time basking in the sun during the winter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[75]</a></sup> Saltwater crocodiles, however, are among the most active of all crocodilians, spending more time cruising and active, especially in water. They are much less <a href="/wiki/Terrestrial_animal" title="Terrestrial animal">terrestrial</a> than most species of crocodiles, spending less time on land except for <a href="/wiki/Thermoregulation" title="Thermoregulation">basking</a>. At times, they tend to spend weeks at sea in search of land and in some cases, <a href="/wiki/Barnacle" title="Barnacle">barnacles</a> have been observed growing on crocodile scales, indicative of the long periods they spend at sea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[76]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Crocodylus_porosus_with_GPS-based_satellite_transmitter_attached_to_the_nuchal_rosette_-_journal.pone.0062127.g002.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="391" data-file-width="556" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Crocodylus_porosus_with_GPS-based_satellite_transmitter_attached_to_the_nuchal_rosette_-_journal.pone.0062127.g002.png/220px-Crocodylus_porosus_with_GPS-based_satellite_transmitter_attached_to_the_nuchal_rosette_-_journal.pone.0062127.g002.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Crocodylus_porosus_with_GPS-based_satellite_transmitter_attached_to_the_nuchal_rosette_-_journal.pone.0062127.g002.png/330px-Crocodylus_porosus_with_GPS-based_satellite_transmitter_attached_to_the_nuchal_rosette_-_journal.pone.0062127.g002.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Crocodylus_porosus_with_GPS-based_satellite_transmitter_attached_to_the_nuchal_rosette_-_journal.pone.0062127.g002.png/440px-Crocodylus_porosus_with_GPS-based_satellite_transmitter_attached_to_the_nuchal_rosette_-_journal.pone.0062127.g002.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Crocodylus_porosus_with_GPS-based_satellite_transmitter_attached_to_the_nuchal_rosette_-_journal.pone.0062127.g002.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Saltwater crocodile with a GPS-based <a href="/wiki/Animal_migration_tracking" title="Animal migration tracking">satellite transmitter</a> attached to its head for tracking</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Despite their relative lethargy, saltwater crocodiles are agile predators and display surprising agility and speed when necessary, usually during strikes at prey. They are capable of explosive bursts of speed when launching an attack from the water. They can also swim at 15 to 18 mph (24 to 29 km/h) in short bursts, around three times as fast as the fastest human swimmers, but when cruising, they usually go at 2 to 3 mph (3.2 to 4.8 km/h). However, stories of crocodiles being faster than a race horse for short distances across land are little more than urban legend. At the water's edge, however, where they can combine propulsion from both feet and tail, their speed can be explosive.</p>
<p>While crocodilian brains are much smaller than those of mammals (as low as 0.05% of body weight in the saltwater crocodile), saltwater crocodiles are capable of learning difficult tasks with very little conditioning, learning to track the migratory route of their prey as the seasons change, and may possess a deeper communication ability than currently accepted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[78]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hunting_and_diet">Hunting and diet</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Saltwater_crocodile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Hunting and diet">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Crocodile_in_Broome_Western_Australia.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="685" data-file-width="1024" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Crocodile_in_Broome_Western_Australia.jpg/220px-Crocodile_in_Broome_Western_Australia.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Crocodile_in_Broome_Western_Australia.jpg/330px-Crocodile_in_Broome_Western_Australia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Crocodile_in_Broome_Western_Australia.jpg/440px-Crocodile_in_Broome_Western_Australia.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Crocodile_in_Broome_Western_Australia.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Feeding young adult in captivity, Western Australia</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Like most species in the crocodilians family, saltwater crocodiles are not fastidious in their choice of food, and readily vary their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prey" title="Prey">prey</a> selection according to availability, nor are they voracious, as they are able to survive on relatively little food for a prolonged period. Because of their size and distribution, saltwater crocodiles hunt the broadest range of prey species of any modern crocodilian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ross_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ross-8">[8]</a></sup> The diet of hatchling, juvenile and subadult saltwater crocodiles has been subject to extensively greater scientific study than that of fully-grown crocodiles, in large part due to the aggression, territoriality and size of adults which make them difficult for biologists to handle without significant risk to safety, for both humans and the crocodiles themselves; the main method used for capturing adult saltwater crocodiles is a huge pole with large hooks meant for shark capture which restrict the crocodile's jaws but can cause damage to their snouts and even this is unproven to allow successful capture for crocodiles in excess of 4 m (13 ft 1 in). While for example 20th century biological studies rigorously cataloged the stomach contents of "sacrificed" adult <a href="/wiki/Nile_crocodile" title="Nile crocodile">Nile crocodiles</a> in <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cott_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cott-80">[79]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beard_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Beard-81">[80]</a></sup> few such studies were done on behalf of saltwater crocodiles despite the plethora that were slaughtered due to the leather trade during that time period. Therefore, the diet of adults is more likely to be based on reliable eye-witness accounts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Webb.2C_G._J._1991_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-Webb.2C_G._J._1991-82">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davenport_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Davenport-83">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[83]</a></sup> Hatchlings are restricted to feeding on smaller animals, such as small fish, frogs, insects and small aquatic <a href="/wiki/Invertebrate" title="Invertebrate">invertebrates</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davenport_83-1"><a href="#cite_note-Davenport-83">[82]</a></sup> In addition to these prey, juveniles also take a variety of freshwater and saltwater fish, various <a href="/wiki/Amphibian" title="Amphibian">amphibians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crustacean" title="Crustacean">crustaceans</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mollusc" title="Mollusc">molluscs</a>, such as large <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gastropod" title="Gastropod">gastropods</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cephalopod" title="Cephalopod">cephalopods</a>, birds, small to medium-sized <a href="/wiki/Mammal" title="Mammal">mammals</a>, and other reptiles, such as snakes and <a href="/wiki/Lizard" title="Lizard">lizards</a>. When crocodiles obtain a length of more than 1.2 m (3.9 ft), the significance of small invertebrate prey fades in favor of small vertebrates including fish and smaller mammals and birds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[84]</a></sup> The larger the animal grows, the greater the variety of its diet, although relatively small <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prey" title="Prey">prey</a> are taken throughout its lifetime.</p>
<p>Among crustacean prey, large <a href="/wiki/Scylla_serrata" title="Scylla serrata">mud crabs</a> of the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Scylla_(genus)" title="Scylla (genus)">Scylla</a></i> are frequently consumed, especially in mangrove habitats. Ground-living birds, such as the <a href="/wiki/Emu" title="Emu">emu</a> (<i>Dromaius novaehollandiae</i>) and different kinds of <a href="/wiki/Water_bird" title="Water bird">water birds</a>, especially the <a href="/wiki/Magpie_goose" title="Magpie goose">magpie goose</a> (<i>Anseranas semipalmata</i>), are the most commonly preyed upon birds, due to the increased chance of encounter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[85]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Messel_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-Messel-87">[86]</a></sup> Even swift-flying birds and <a href="/wiki/Bat" title="Bat">bats</a> may be snatched if close to the surface of water,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ross_8-3"><a href="#cite_note-Ross-8">[8]</a></sup> as well as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wading_bird" title="Wading bird">wading birds</a> while these are patrolling the shore looking for food, even down to the size of a <a href="/wiki/Common_sandpiper" title="Common sandpiper">common sandpiper</a> (<i>Actitis hypoleucos</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NewHolland_38-3"><a href="#cite_note-NewHolland-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[87]</a></sup> Mammalian prey of juveniles and subadults are usually as large as the smaller species of <a href="/wiki/Ungulate" title="Ungulate">ungulates</a>, such as the <a href="/wiki/Greater_mouse-deer" title="Greater mouse-deer">greater mouse-deer</a> (<i>Tragulus napu</i>) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hog_deer" title="Hog deer">hog deer</a> (<i>Hyelaphus porcinus</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[88]</a></sup> Various <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mammalian" title="Mammalian">mammalian</a> species including, monkeys (i.e. <a href="/wiki/Crab-eating_macaque" title="Crab-eating macaque">crab-eating macaques</a> (<i>Macaca fascicularis</i>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[89]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Long-tailed_macaque" title="Long-tailed macaque">long-tailed macaques</a> (<i>M. fascicularis</i>) &amp; <a href="/wiki/Proboscis_monkey" title="Proboscis monkey">proboscis monkeys</a> (<i>Nasalis larvatus</i>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[90]</a></sup>), <a href="/wiki/Gibbon" title="Gibbon">gibbons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Porcupine" title="Porcupine">porcupines</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wallaby" title="Wallaby">wallabies</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[91]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mongoose" title="Mongoose">mongoose</a>, <a href="/wiki/Civet" title="Civet">civets</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jackal" title="Jackal">jackals</a> (<i>Canis</i> ssp.), turtles, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flying_fox" title="Flying fox">flying foxes</a> (<i>Pteropus</i> ssp.), <a href="/wiki/Hare" title="Hare">hares</a> (<i>Lepus</i> ssp.), <a href="/wiki/Rodent" title="Rodent">rodents</a>, <a href="/wiki/Badger" title="Badger">badgers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Otter" title="Otter">otters</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[92]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Fishing_cat" title="Fishing cat">fishing cats</a> (<i>Prionailurus viverrinus</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Chevrotain" title="Chevrotain">chevrotains</a> are readily taken when encountered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Corlett_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Corlett-94">[93]</a></sup> Unlike fish, crabs and aquatic creatures, mammals and birds are usually found only sporadically in or next to water so crocodiles seem to search for places where such prey may be concentrated, i.e. the water under a tree holding a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flying_fox" title="Flying fox">flying fox</a> colony or spots where herds of water buffaloes feed, in order to capture small animals disturbed by the buffalo or (if a large adult crocodile is hunting) weaker members of the buffalo herd.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Messel_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-Messel-87">[86]</a></sup> Studies have shown that unlike <a href="/wiki/Freshwater_crocodile" title="Freshwater crocodile">freshwater crocodiles</a> (which can easily be killed by toads), saltwater crocodiles are partially resistant to <a href="/wiki/Cane_toad" title="Cane toad">cane toad</a> (<i>Rhinella marina</i>) toxins and can consume them but only in small quantities and not enough to provide effective natural control for this virulent introduced pest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[94]</a></sup> Large crocodiles, even the oldest males, do not ignore small species, especially those without developed escape abilities, when the opportunity arises. On the other hand, sub-adult saltwater crocodiles weighing only 8.7 to 15.8 kg (19 to 35 lb) (and measuring 1.36 to 1.79 m (4 ft 6 in to 5 ft 10 in)) have been recorded killing and eating <a href="/wiki/Goat" title="Goat">goats</a> (<i>Capra aegagrus hircus</i>) weighing 50 to 92% of their own body mass in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orissa,_India" title="Orissa, India">Orissa, India</a>, so are capable of attacking large prey from an early age.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Corlett_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-Corlett-94">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[96]</a></sup> It was found the diet of specimens in juvenile to subadult range, since they feed on any animals up to their own size practically no matter how small, was more diverse than adults who often ignored all prey below a certain size limit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[97]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Crocodylus_porosus_feeding_frenzy_by_Gregg_Yan_01.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="1508" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Crocodylus_porosus_feeding_frenzy_by_Gregg_Yan_01.jpg/220px-Crocodylus_porosus_feeding_frenzy_by_Gregg_Yan_01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Crocodylus_porosus_feeding_frenzy_by_Gregg_Yan_01.jpg/330px-Crocodylus_porosus_feeding_frenzy_by_Gregg_Yan_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Crocodylus_porosus_feeding_frenzy_by_Gregg_Yan_01.jpg/440px-Crocodylus_porosus_feeding_frenzy_by_Gregg_Yan_01.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Crocodylus_porosus_feeding_frenzy_by_Gregg_Yan_01.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i>C. porosus</i> tearing apart a pig carcass for consumption</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Large animals taken by adult crocodiles include <a href="/wiki/Sambar_deer" title="Sambar deer">sambar deer</a> (<i>Rusa unicolor</i>), <a href="/wiki/Wild_boar" title="Wild boar">wild boar</a> (<i>Sus scrofa</i>), <a href="/wiki/Malayan_tapir" title="Malayan tapir">Malayan tapirs</a> (<i>Tapirus indicus</i>), <a href="/wiki/Kangaroo" title="Kangaroo">kangaroos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Orangutan" title="Orangutan">orangutans</a> (<i>Pongo</i> ssp.), <a href="/wiki/Dingo" title="Dingo">dingos</a> (<i>Canis lupus dingo</i>), <a href="/wiki/Tiger" title="Tiger">tigers</a> (<i>Panthera tigris</i>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[98]</a></sup> and large bovines, such as <a href="/wiki/Banteng" title="Banteng">banteng</a> (<i>Bos javanicus</i>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leach_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-Leach-100">[99]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Water_buffalo" title="Water buffalo">water buffalo</a> (<i>Bubalus arnee</i>), and <a href="/wiki/Gaur" title="Gaur">gaur</a> (<i>Bos gaurus</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kumar_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kumar-73">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Collins_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-Collins-101">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[104]</a></sup> However, larger animals are only sporadically taken due to the fact only large males typically attack very large prey and large ungulates and other sizeable wild mammals are only sparsely distributed in this species' range, outside of a few key areas such as the <a href="/wiki/Sundarbans" title="Sundarbans">Sundarbans</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ross_8-4"><a href="#cite_note-Ross-8">[8]</a></sup> Off-setting this, <a href="/wiki/Goat" title="Goat">goats</a>, <a href="/wiki/Water_buffalo" title="Water buffalo">water buffalo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wild_boar" title="Wild boar">wild boar</a>/<a href="/wiki/Pig" title="Pig">pigs</a> have been introduced to many of the areas occupied by saltwater crocodiles and returned to feral states to varying degrees and thus can amply support large crocodiles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guggisberg_26-3"><a href="#cite_note-Guggisberg-26">[26]</a></sup> Any type of <a href="/wiki/Livestock" title="Livestock">domestic livestock</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Chicken" title="Chicken">chicken</a> (<i>Gallus gallus domesticus</i>), <a href="/wiki/Sheep" title="Sheep">sheep</a> (<i>Ovis aries</i>), pigs, <a href="/wiki/Horse" title="Horse">horses</a> (<i>Equus ferus caballus</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Cattle" title="Cattle">cattle</a> (<i>Bos primigenius taurus</i>), and domesticated animals/pets may be eaten if given the opportunity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ross_8-5"><a href="#cite_note-Ross-8">[8]</a></sup> As a seagoing species, the saltwater crocodile also preys on a variety of saltwater <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bony_fish" title="Bony fish">bony fish</a> and other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marine_animal" title="Marine animal">marine animals</a>, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sea_snake" title="Sea snake">sea snakes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sea_turtle" title="Sea turtle">sea turtles</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sea_bird" title="Sea bird">sea birds</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dugong" title="Dugong">dugongs</a> (<i>Dugong dugon</i>), <a href="/wiki/Batoidea" title="Batoidea">rays</a> (including large <a href="/wiki/Sawfish" title="Sawfish">sawfish</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[105]</a></sup>), and small <a href="/wiki/Shark" title="Shark">sharks</a>. Most witnessed acts of predation on marine animals have occurred in coastal waters or within sight of land, with female sea turtles and their babies caught during mating season when the turtles are closer to shore and bull sharks being the only largish shark with a strong propensity to patrol brackish and fresh waters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ross_8-6"><a href="#cite_note-Ross-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wood_35-4"><a href="#cite_note-Wood-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[108]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[109]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[112]</a></sup> However, there is evidence that saltwater crocodile do hunt while out in the open seas, based upon the remains of pelagic fishes that only dwell miles away from land being found in their stomachs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Callaway.2C_J._M._1997_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-Callaway.2C_J._M._1997-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Blaber.2C_S._J._2008_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-Blaber.2C_S._J._2008-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>The hunting methods utilized by saltwater crocodiles are indistinct from any other crocodilian, with the hunting crocodile submerging and quietly swimming over to the prey before pouncing upwards striking suddenly. Unlike some other crocodilians, such as <a href="/wiki/Alligator" title="Alligator">alligators</a> and even <a href="/wiki/Nile_crocodile" title="Nile crocodile">Nile crocodiles</a>, they are not known to have hunted on dry land.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ross_8-7"><a href="#cite_note-Ross-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guggisberg_26-4"><a href="#cite_note-Guggisberg-26">[26]</a></sup> Young saltwater crocodiles are capable of breaching their entire body into their air in a single upward motion while hunting prey that may be perched on low hanging branches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davenport_83-2"><a href="#cite_note-Davenport-83">[82]</a></sup> While hunting <a href="/wiki/Rhesus_macaque" title="Rhesus macaque">rhesus macaques</a>, crocodiles have been seen to knock the monkeys off a bank by knocking them with their tail, forcing the macaque into water for easy consumption. However, whether tail use in hunting is intentional or just an accidental benefit is not definitely clear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guggisberg_26-5"><a href="#cite_note-Guggisberg-26">[26]</a></sup> As with other crocodilians, their sharp, peg-like teeth are well-suited to seize and tightly grip prey, but not designed to <a href="/wiki/Cutting" title="Cutting">shear</a> flesh. Small prey are simply swallowed whole, while larger animals are forcibly dragged into deep water and <a href="/wiki/Drowning" title="Drowning">drowned</a> or crushed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-news.nationalgeographic.com_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-news.nationalgeographic.com-114">[113]</a></sup> Large prey is then torn into manageable pieces by "death rolling" (the spinning of the crocodile to twist off hunks of meat) or by sudden jerks of the head.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[114]</a></sup> Occasionally, food items will be stored for later consumption once a crocodile eats its fill, although this can lead to scavenging by interlopers such as monitor lizards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[115]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Bite">Bite</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Saltwater_crocodile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Bite">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Saltwater_Crocodile_(Crocodylus_porosus)_(8851846180).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="3072" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Saltwater_Crocodile_%28Crocodylus_porosus%29_%288851846180%29.jpg/220px-Saltwater_Crocodile_%28Crocodylus_porosus%29_%288851846180%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Saltwater_Crocodile_%28Crocodylus_porosus%29_%288851846180%29.jpg/330px-Saltwater_Crocodile_%28Crocodylus_porosus%29_%288851846180%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Saltwater_Crocodile_%28Crocodylus_porosus%29_%288851846180%29.jpg/440px-Saltwater_Crocodile_%28Crocodylus_porosus%29_%288851846180%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Saltwater_Crocodile_(Crocodylus_porosus)_(8851846180).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Saltwater crocodiles, even adults, can breach out of the water in upwards direction to capture food, although are most often seen to do so when enticed by bait as seen here.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Saltwater crocodiles hold the record for the highest <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bite" title="Bite">bite force</a> ever recorded in any animal, with a peak bite force of 16,414 N (3,690 lbf), far surpassing the highest recorded value in the <a href="/wiki/Spotted_hyena" title="Spotted hyena">spotted hyena</a> of 4,500 N (1,012 lbf).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-erickson_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-erickson-117">[116]</a></sup> The extraordinary bite of crocodilians is a result of their <a href="/wiki/Anatomy" title="Anatomy">anatomy</a>. The space for the jaw muscle in the <a href="/wiki/Skull" title="Skull">skull</a> is very large, which is easily visible from the outside as a bulge at each side. The nature of the <a href="/wiki/Muscle" title="Muscle">muscle</a> is extremely stiff, almost as hard as bone to the touch, such that it can appear to be the continuum of the skull. Another trait is that most of the muscle in a crocodile's jaw is arranged for clamping down. Despite the strong muscles to close the jaw, crocodiles have extremely small and weak muscles to open the jaw. The jaws of a crocodile can be securely shut with several layers of <a href="/wiki/Duct_tape" title="Duct tape">duct tape</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[117]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Reproduction">Reproduction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Saltwater_crocodile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Reproduction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hatchling_saltwater_crocodile.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="3120" height="141" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Hatchling_saltwater_crocodile.jpg/220px-Hatchling_saltwater_crocodile.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Hatchling_saltwater_crocodile.jpg/330px-Hatchling_saltwater_crocodile.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Hatchling_saltwater_crocodile.jpg/440px-Hatchling_saltwater_crocodile.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hatchling_saltwater_crocodile.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Saltwater crocodiles go through numerous physiological changes as they mature. Pictured here is a hatchling age or baby crocodile.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Saltwater_Crocodile_(Crocodylus_porosus)_juvenile_(8067785958).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="4000" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Saltwater_Crocodile_%28Crocodylus_porosus%29_juvenile_%288067785958%29.jpg/220px-Saltwater_Crocodile_%28Crocodylus_porosus%29_juvenile_%288067785958%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Saltwater_Crocodile_%28Crocodylus_porosus%29_juvenile_%288067785958%29.jpg/330px-Saltwater_Crocodile_%28Crocodylus_porosus%29_juvenile_%288067785958%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Saltwater_Crocodile_%28Crocodylus_porosus%29_juvenile_%288067785958%29.jpg/440px-Saltwater_Crocodile_%28Crocodylus_porosus%29_juvenile_%288067785958%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Saltwater_Crocodile_(Crocodylus_porosus)_juvenile_(8067785958).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
