Seen here is a juvenile crocodile, which grows considerably in length over several years but is easily distinguished by slender build and size.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Saltwater_crocodile_(Crocodylus_porosus),_Gembira_Loka_Zoo,_2015-03-15_01.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1477" data-file-width="2218" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Saltwater_crocodile_%28Crocodylus_porosus%29%2C_Gembira_Loka_Zoo%2C_2015-03-15_01.jpg/220px-Saltwater_crocodile_%28Crocodylus_porosus%29%2C_Gembira_Loka_Zoo%2C_2015-03-15_01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Saltwater_crocodile_%28Crocodylus_porosus%29%2C_Gembira_Loka_Zoo%2C_2015-03-15_01.jpg/330px-Saltwater_crocodile_%28Crocodylus_porosus%29%2C_Gembira_Loka_Zoo%2C_2015-03-15_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Saltwater_crocodile_%28Crocodylus_porosus%29%2C_Gembira_Loka_Zoo%2C_2015-03-15_01.jpg/440px-Saltwater_crocodile_%28Crocodylus_porosus%29%2C_Gembira_Loka_Zoo%2C_2015-03-15_01.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Saltwater_crocodile_(Crocodylus_porosus),_Gembira_Loka_Zoo,_2015-03-15_01.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A saltwater crocodile in the subadult age range at Gembira Loka Zoo, similar but not as robust and relatively small headed compared to adults.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Saltwater crocodiles mate in the wet season, when water levels are at their highest. In Australia, the male and female engage in courtship in September and October, and the female lays eggs between November and March.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leach_100-1"><a href="#cite_note-Leach-100">[99]</a></sup> It is possible the rising temperatures of the wet season provoke reproductive behaviour in this species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Messel_87-2"><a href="#cite_note-Messel-87">[86]</a></sup> While crocodilians generally nest every year, there have been several recorded cases of female saltwater crocodiles nesting only every other year and also records of a female attempting to produce two broods in a single wet season.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Messel_87-3"><a href="#cite_note-Messel-87">[86]</a></sup> The female selects the nesting site, and both parents will defend the nesting territory, which is typically a stretch of shore along tidal rivers or freshwater areas, especially swamps. Nests are often in surprisingly exposed location, often in mud with little to no vegetation around and thus limited protection from the sun and wind. The nest is a mound of mud and vegetation, usually measuring 175 cm (69 in) long and 53 cm (21 in) high, with an entrance averaging 160 cm (63 in) in diameter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Messel_87-4"><a href="#cite_note-Messel-87">[86]</a></sup> Some nests in unlikely habitats have occurred, such as rocky rubble or in a damp low-grass field.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[119]</a></sup> The female crocodile usually scratches a layer of leaves and other debris around the nest entrance and this covering is reported to produce an "astonishing" amount of warmth for the eggs (conicidentially these nesting habits are similar to those of the birds known as <a href="/wiki/Megapode" title="Megapode">megapodes</a> that nest in upland areas of the same <a href="/wiki/Australasia" title="Australasia">Australasian</a> regions where saltwater crocodiles are found).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guggisberg_26-6"><a href="#cite_note-Guggisberg-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[120]</a></sup> The female typically lays from 40 to 60 eggs, but some clutches have included up to 90. The eggs measure on average 8 by 5 cm (3.1 by 2.0 in) and weigh 113 g (4.0 oz) on average in Australia and 121 g (4.3 oz) in <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leach_100-2"><a href="#cite_note-Leach-100">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gopi_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gopi-122">[121]</a></sup> These are relatively small, as the average female saltwater crocodile weighs around five times as much as a freshwater crocodile, but lays eggs that are only about 20% larger in measurement and 40% heavier than those of the smaller species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NewHolland_38-4"><a href="#cite_note-NewHolland-38">[38]</a></sup> The average weight of a new hatchling in Australia is reportedly 69.4 g (2.45 oz).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leach_100-3"><a href="#cite_note-Leach-100">[99]</a></sup> Although the female guards the nest for 80 to 98 days (in extreme high and low cases from 75 to 106 days), the loss of eggs is often high due to flooding and occasionally to predation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leach_100-4"><a href="#cite_note-Leach-100">[99]</a></sup> As in all crocodilians, the sex of the hatchlings is determined by temperature. At 28–30 degrees all hatchlings will be female, at 30–32 degrees 86% of hatchlings are male, and at 33 or more degrees predominantly female (84%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[122]</a></sup> In Australia, <a href="/wiki/Goanna" title="Goanna">goannas</a> (<i>Varanus giganteus</i>) commonly eat freshwater crocodile eggs (feeding on up to 95% of clutch if discovered), but are relatively unlikely to eat saltwater crocodile eggs due to the vigilance of the imposing mother, with about 25% of the eggs being lost to goannas (less than half as many Nile crocodile eggs are estimated to be eaten by monitors in Africa).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NewHolland_38-5"><a href="#cite_note-NewHolland-38">[38]</a></sup> A majority of the loss of eggs in the saltwater crocodile occurs due to flooding of the nest hole.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Messel_87-5"><a href="#cite_note-Messel-87">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Magnusson_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-Magnusson-124">[123]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Female_croc.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="920" data-file-width="1468" height="138" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Female_croc.jpg/220px-Female_croc.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Female_croc.jpg/330px-Female_croc.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cb/Female_croc.jpg/440px-Female_croc.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Female_croc.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Wild adult female saltwater crocodile.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>As in all crocodilian species, the female saltwater crocodile exhibits a remarkable level of maternal care for a reptile. She excavates the nest in response to "yelping" calls from the hatchlings, and even gently rolls eggs in her mouth to assist hatching. The female will then carry the hatchlings to water in her mouth (as <a href="/wiki/Nile_crocodile#Reproduction" title="Nile crocodile">Nile crocodile</a> and <a href="/wiki/American_alligator#Reproduction" title="American alligator">American alligator</a> females have been observed doing when their eggs hatch) and remains with the young for several months. Despite her diligence, losses of baby crocodiles are heavy due to various predators and unrelated crocodiles of their own species. Only approximately 1% of the hatchlings will survive to adulthood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-125">[124]</a></sup> By crocodilian standards, saltwater crocodile hatchlings are exceptionally aggressive to one another and will often fight almost immediately after being transported to water by their mother.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[125]</a></sup> The young naturally start to disperse after around 8 months, and start to exhibit territorial behaviour at around 2.5-year-old. They are the most territorial of extant crocodilians and, due to their aggressiveness to conspecifics, from the dispersed immature stage on, they are never seen in concentrations or loose groups as are most other crocodilians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[126]</a></sup> However, even females will not reach proper sexual maturity for another 10 years. Saltwater crocodiles that survive to adulthood can attain a very long <a href="/wiki/Maximum_life_span" title="Maximum life span">lifespan</a>, with an estimated life expectancy upwards of 70 years, and some individuals possibly exceed 100 years, although no such extreme ages have been verified for any crocodilian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britton_37-2"><a href="#cite_note-Britton-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[127]</a></sup> While adults have few predators, baby saltwater crocodiles may fall prey to <a href="/wiki/Monitor_lizard" title="Monitor lizard">monitor lizards</a> (occasionally but not commonly the numerous <a href="/wiki/Goanna" title="Goanna">goanna</a> in Australia and the <a href="/wiki/Asian_water_monitor" title="Asian water monitor">Asian water monitor</a> (<i>Varanus salvator</i>) further north), predatory fish (especially the <a href="/wiki/Barramundi" title="Barramundi">barramundi</a> (<i>Lates calcarifer</i>)), <a href="/wiki/Wild_boar" title="Wild boar">wild boars</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rat" title="Rat">rats</a>, various aquatic and raptorial birds (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Black-necked_stork" title="Black-necked stork">black-necked storks</a> (<i>Ephippiorhynchus asiaticus</i>) and <a href="/wiki/White-bellied_sea_eagle" title="White-bellied sea eagle">white-bellied sea eagles</a> (<i>Haliaeetus leucogaster</i>)), <a href="/wiki/Python_(genus)" title="Python (genus)">pythons</a>, larger crocodiles, and many other predators.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Messel_87-6"><a href="#cite_note-Messel-87">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Leach_100-5"><a href="#cite_note-Leach-100">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gopi_122-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gopi-122">[121]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Magnusson_124-1"><a href="#cite_note-Magnusson-124">[123]</a></sup> Pigs and cattle also occasionally inadvertently trample eggs and nests on occasion and degrade habitat quality where found in numbers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[128]</a></sup> Juveniles may also fall prey to tigers and <a href="/wiki/Leopard" title="Leopard">leopards</a> (<i>Panthera pardus</i>) in certain parts of their range, although encounters between these predators are rare and cats are likely to avoid areas with saltwater crocodiles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[129]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Conservation_status">Conservation status</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Saltwater_crocodile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Conservation status">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Saltwater_crocodile.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2112" data-file-width="2816" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Saltwater_crocodile.jpg/220px-Saltwater_crocodile.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Saltwater_crocodile.jpg/330px-Saltwater_crocodile.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Saltwater_crocodile.jpg/440px-Saltwater_crocodile.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Saltwater_crocodile.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Saltwater crocodile</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In addition to being hunted for its meat and eggs, the saltwater crocodile has the most commercially valuable skin of any crocodilian; and unregulated hunting during the 20th century caused a dramatic decline in the species throughout its range, with the population in northern Australia reduced 95% by 1971. The years from 1940 to 1970 were the peak of unregulated hunting and may have regionally caused irreparable damage to saltwater crocodile populations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Whitaker2_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-Whitaker2-60">[59]</a></sup> The species currently has full legal protection in all Australian states and territories where it is found – Western Australia (since 1970), Northern Territory (since 1971) and Queensland (since 1974).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[130]</a></sup> Illegal hunting still persists in some areas, with protection in some countries being grossly ineffective, and trade is often difficult to monitor and control over such a vast range. Despite this, the species has made a dramatic recovery in recent decades. Because of its resurgence, the species is considered of minimal concern for extinction. However, many areas have not recovered, some population surveys have shown that although young crocodiles are present, fewer than 10% of specimens spotted are in adult size range and no particularly large males, such as <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">Sri Lanka</a> or the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Republic_of_Palau" title="Republic of Palau">Republic of Palau</a>. This is indicative of both potential continued persecution and exploitation and a non-recovered breeding population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[131]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[132]</a></sup> In a more balanced population, such as those from <a href="/wiki/Bhitarkanika_National_Park" title="Bhitarkanika National Park">Bhitarkanika National Park</a> or <a href="/wiki/Sabah" title="Sabah">Sabah</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">Malaysia</a>, 28% and 24.2% of specimens observed were in the adult size range of more than 3 m (9 ft 10 in).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stuebing.2C_R._B._1994_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-Stuebing.2C_R._B._1994-67">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[133]</a></sup></p>
<p>Currently, the species is listed in <a href="/wiki/CITES" title="CITES">CITES</a> as follows:</p>
<ul>
<li>Appendix I (prohibiting all commercial trade in the species or its byproducts): All wild populations except for those of Australia, Indonesia and Papua New Guinea</li>
<li>Appendix II (commercial trade allowed with export permit; import permits may or may not be required depending on the laws of the importing country): Australia, Indonesia and Papua New Guinea wild populations, plus all worldwide populations bred in captivity for commercial purposes</li>
</ul>
<p>Habitat loss continues to be a major problem. In northern Australia, much of the nesting habitat of the saltwater crocodile is susceptible to trampling by feral water buffalo, although buffalo eradication programs have now reduced this problem considerably. Even where large areas of suitable habitat remain, subtle habitat alterations can be a problem, such as in the <a href="/wiki/Andaman_Islands" title="Andaman Islands">Andaman Islands</a>, where freshwater areas, used for nesting, are being increasingly converted to human agriculture. After the commercial value of crocodile skins waned, perhaps the greatest immediate challenge to implementing conservation efforts has been the occasional danger the species can be to humans and the resulting negative view of the crocodile.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_125-1"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-125">[124]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[134]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Relationship_with_humans">Relationship with humans</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Saltwater_crocodile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Relationship with humans">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Attacks_on_humans">Attacks on humans</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Saltwater_crocodile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Attacks on humans">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Of all the crocodilians, the saltwater crocodile has the strongest tendency to treat humans as prey, and has a long history of attacking humans who unknowingly stray into its territory. As a result of its power, intimidating size and speed, survival of a direct predatory attack is unlikely if the crocodile is able to make direct contact. By contrast to the American policy of encouraging a certain degree of habitat coexistence with alligators, the only recommended policy for dealing with saltwater crocodiles is to completely avoid their habitat whenever possible, as they are exceedingly aggressive when encroached upon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guggisberg_26-7"><a href="#cite_note-Guggisberg-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kakadu_2430.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="700" data-file-width="554" height="215" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Kakadu_2430.jpg/170px-Kakadu_2430.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Kakadu_2430.jpg/255px-Kakadu_2430.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Kakadu_2430.jpg/340px-Kakadu_2430.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kakadu_2430.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
No Swimming sign used by <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Parks_and_Wildlife_Commission_of_the_Northern_Territory&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Parks and Wildlife Commission of the Northern Territory (page does not exist)">Parks and Wildlife Commission of the Northern Territory</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[135]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Exact data on attacks are limited outside Australia, where one or two fatal attacks are reported per year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Caldicott_et_al._2005_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-Caldicott_et_al._2005-137">[136]</a></sup> From 1971 to 2013, the total number of fatalities reported in Australia due to saltwater crocodile attack was 106.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[137]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Caldicott_et_al._2005_137-1"><a href="#cite_note-Caldicott_et_al._2005-137">[136]</a></sup> The low level of attacks may be due to extensive efforts by wildlife officials in Australia to post crocodile warning signs at numerous at-risk billabongs, rivers, lakes and beaches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[138]</a></sup> Also, recent, less-publicised attacks have been reported in <a href="/wiki/Borneo" title="Borneo">Borneo</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[139]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sumatra" title="Sumatra">Sumatra</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[140]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/East_India" title="East India">Eastern India</a> (<a href="/wiki/Andaman_Islands" title="Andaman Islands">Andaman Islands</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[141]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[142]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Burma" title="Burma">Burma</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[143]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Sarawak" title="Sarawak">Sarawak</a>, <a href="/wiki/Borneo" title="Borneo">Borneo</a>, the average number of fatal attacks is reportedly 2.8 annually for the years from 2000 to 2003.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AttackResearch_145-0"><a href="#cite_note-AttackResearch-145">[144]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Territory" title="Northern Territory">Northern Territory</a> in Australia, attempts have made to relocate saltwater crocodiles who've displayed aggressive behaviour towards humans but these have proven ineffective as the problem crocodiles are apparently able to find their way back towards their original territories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[145]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Darwin,_Northern_Territory" title="Darwin, Northern Territory">Darwin area</a> from 2007–2009, 67–78% of "problem crocodiles" were identified as males.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[146]</a></sup></p>
<p>Many attacks in areas outside Australia are believed to go unreported, with one study positing up to 20 to 30 attacks occur every year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AttackResearch_145-1"><a href="#cite_note-AttackResearch-145">[144]</a></sup> This number may be conservative in light of several areas where humans and saltwater crocodiles co-exist in relatively undeveloped, low-economy and rural regions, where attacks are likely to go unreported.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[147]</a></sup> However, claims in the past that saltwater crocodiles are responsible for thousands of human fatalities annually are likely to have been exaggerations and were probably falsified to benefit leather companies, hunting organizations and other sources which may have benefited from maximizing the negative perception of crocodiles for financial gain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guggisberg_26-8"><a href="#cite_note-Guggisberg-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wood_35-5"><a href="#cite_note-Wood-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AttackResearch_145-2"><a href="#cite_note-AttackResearch-145">[144]</a></sup> Although it does not pay to underestimate such a formidable predator, one that has no reason to not view humans as prey, many wild saltwater crocodiles are normally quite wary of humans and will go out of their way to submerge and swim away from them, even in large adult males, if previously subject to harassment or persecution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[148]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[149]</a></sup> Some attacks on humans appear to be territorial rather than predatory in nature, with crocodiles over two years in age often attacking anything that comes into their area (including boats). Humans can usually escape alive from such encounters, which comprise about half of all attacks. Non-fatal attacks usually involve crocodiles of 3 m (9 ft 10 in) or less in length. Fatal attacks, more likely to be predatory in motivation, commonly involve larger crocodiles with an average estimated size of 4.3 m (14 ft 1 in). Under normal circumstances, <a href="/wiki/Nile_crocodile" title="Nile crocodile">Nile crocodiles</a> are believed to be responsible for a considerably greater number of fatal attacks on humans than saltwater crocodiles, but this may have more to do with the fact that many people in Africa tend to rely on riparian areas for their livelihood, which is less prevalent in most of Asia and certainly less so in Australia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AttackResearch_145-3"><a href="#cite_note-AttackResearch-145">[144]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Andaman_Islands" title="Andaman Islands">Andaman Islands</a>, the number of fatal attacks on humans has reportedly increased, the reason inferred due to habitat destruction and reduction of natural prey.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[150]</a></sup></p>
<p>During the Japanese retreat in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Ramree_Island" title="Battle of Ramree Island">Battle of Ramree Island</a> on 19 February 1945, saltwater crocodiles may have been responsible for the deaths of over 400 Japanese soldiers. British soldiers encircled the swampland through which the Japanese were retreating, condemning the Japanese to a night in the mangroves, which were home to thousands of saltwater crocodiles. Many Japanese soldiers did not survive this night, but their death mainly as a result of crocodile attacks has been doubted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[151]</a></sup> Another reported mass attack reportedly involve a cruise in eastern <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> where a boat accident forced 28 people into the water where they were reportedly consumed by saltwater crocodiles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wood_35-6"><a href="#cite_note-Wood-35">[35]</a></sup> Another notorious crocodile attack was in 1985, on ecofeminist <a href="/wiki/Val_Plumwood" title="Val Plumwood">Val Plumwood</a>, who survived the attack.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[152]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-154"><a href="#cite_note-154">[153]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_references">Cultural references</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Saltwater_crocodile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Cultural references">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Australianstamp_1535.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="717" data-file-width="591" height="206" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Australianstamp_1535.jpg/170px-Australianstamp_1535.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Australianstamp_1535.jpg/255px-Australianstamp_1535.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Australianstamp_1535.jpg/340px-Australianstamp_1535.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Australianstamp_1535.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
1948 postage stamp depicting an aboriginal artwork of the saltwater crocodile</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>According to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wondjina" title="Wondjina">Wondjina</a>, the mythology of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indigenous_Australian" title="Indigenous Australian">Indigenous Australians</a>, the saltwater crocodile was banished from the fresh water for becoming full of bad spirits and growing too large, unlike the freshwater crocodile, which was somewhat revered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[154]</a></sup> As such, Aboriginal rock art depicting the saltwater crocodile is rare, although examples of up to 3,000 years old can be found in caves in Kakadu and Arnhem land, roughly matching the species distribution. The species is frequently depicted in contemporary aboriginal art.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[155]</a></sup></p>
<p>The species is featured on several postage stamps, including an 1894 State of North Borneo 12-cent stamp; a 1948 Australian 2 shilling stamp depicting an aboriginal rock artwork of the species; a 1966 Republic of Indonesia stamp; a 1994 Palau 20-cent stamp; a 1997 Australian 22-cent stamp; and a 2005 1 Malaysian ringgit postage stamp.</p>
<p>The species has featured in contemporary Australian film and television including the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%22Crocodile%22_Dundee" title='"Crocodile" Dundee'>"Crocodile" Dundee</a> series of films and <a href="/wiki/The_Crocodile_Hunter" title="The Crocodile Hunter">The Crocodile Hunter</a> television series. There are now several saltwater crocodile-themed parks in Australia.</p>
<p>The crocodile is considered to be holy on <a href="/wiki/Timor" title="Timor">Timor</a>. According to legend, the island was formed by a giant crocodile. The <a href="/wiki/Papuan_people" title="Papuan people">Papuan people</a> have a similar and very involved myth and traditionally the crocodile was described as relative (normally a father or grandfather).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guggisberg_26-9"><a href="#cite_note-Guggisberg-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Examples_of_large_unconfirmed_saltwater_crocodiles">Examples of large unconfirmed saltwater crocodiles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Saltwater_crocodile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Examples of large unconfirmed saltwater crocodiles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Large saltwater crocodiles have always attracted mainstream attention throughout the ages, and have suffered from all sorts of big fish stories and hunter tales, due to man's desire to find the largest of an any given thing. Therefore, the largest size recorded for a saltwater crocodile has always been a subject of considerable controversy. The reason behind unverified sizes is either the case of insufficient/inconclusive data or exaggeration from a folkloric point of view. This section is dedicated to examples of the largest saltwater crocodiles recorded outside scientific norms measurement and estimation, with the aim of satisfying the public interest without creating data pollution, as well as serving an educational purpose of guiding the reader to separate fact from possible fiction. Below, in descending order starting from the largest, are some examples of large unconfirmed saltwater crocodiles, recorded throughout history.</p>
<ul>
<li>A crocodile shot in the <a href="/wiki/Bay_of_Bengal" title="Bay of Bengal">Bay of Bengal</a> in 1840 was reported at 10.1 m (33 ft 2 in). Furthermore, this specimen was claimed to have a belly girth of 4.17 m (13 ft 8 in) and a body mass estimated 3,000 kg (6,600 lb). However, the skull of this specimen was examined by Guinness Records and found to be only 66.5 cm (26.2 in) in length, indicating the above size was considerably exaggerated and the animal would have probably measured no more than 5.89 m (19 ft 4 in).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wood_35-7"><a href="#cite_note-Wood-35">[35]</a></sup></li>
<li>James R. Montgomery, who ran a plantation near to the <a href="/wiki/Lower_Kinabatangan_Segama_Wetlands" title="Lower Kinabatangan Segama Wetlands">Lower Kinabatangan Segama Wetlands</a> in <a href="/wiki/Borneo" title="Borneo">Borneo</a> from 1926 to 1932, claimed to have netted, killed, and examined numerous crocodiles well over 6.1 m (20 ft 0 in) there, including a specimen he claims measured 10 m (32 ft 10 in). However, no one scientifically confirmed any of Montgomery's specimens and no voucher specimens are known.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wood_35-8"><a href="#cite_note-Wood-35">[35]</a></sup></li>
<li>A crocodile shot in Queensland in 1957, nicknamed Krys the croc (named after the woman that shot the crocodile in July 1957; Krystina Pawlowski), was reported to be 8.63 m (28 ft 4 in) long, but no verified measurements were made and no remains of this crocodile exist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[156]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[157]</a></sup> A "replica" of this crocodile has been made as a tourist attraction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[158]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[159]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-161"><a href="#cite_note-161">[160]</a></sup></li>
<li>A crocodile killed in 1823 at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jalajala,_Rizal" title="Jalajala, Rizal">Jalajala</a> on the main island of <a href="/wiki/Luzon" title="Luzon">Luzon</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a> was reported at 8.2 m (26 ft 11 in). However the skull of the specimen is 66.5 cm (26.2 in) long indicating an animal of approximately 6.1 m (20 ft 0 in).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wood_35-9"><a href="#cite_note-Wood-35">[35]</a></sup></li>
<li>The skull of a crocodile shot in <a href="/wiki/Odisha" title="Odisha">Odisha</a>, India,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-underwatertimes1_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-underwatertimes1-162">[161]</a></sup> was claimed to measure 7.6 m (24 ft 11 in) in life, but when given scholarly examination, was thought to have come from a crocodile of a length no greater than 7 m (23 ft 0 in).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[162]</a></sup></li>
<li>A reported 7.6 m (24 ft 11 in) crocodile was killed in the <a href="/wiki/Hooghly_River" title="Hooghly River">Hooghly River</a> in the Alipore District of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Calcutta" title="Calcutta">Calcutta</a>. However, examinations of the animal's skull, one of the largest skulls known to exist for the species at 75 cm (30 in) long, actually indicating it could have measured 6.7 m (22 ft 0 in).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wood_35-10"><a href="#cite_note-Wood-35">[35]</a></sup></li>
<li>In 2006, <a href="/wiki/Guinness_World_Records" title="Guinness World Records">Guinness</a> accepted a 7.1 m (23 ft 4 in), 2,000-kg (4,400-lb) male saltwater crocodile living within <a href="/wiki/Bhitarkanika_National_Park" title="Bhitarkanika National Park">Bhitarkanika Park</a> in Odisha. Due to the difficulty of trapping and measuring a very large living crocodile, the accuracy of these dimensions are yet to be verified. These observations and estimations have been made by park officials over the course of ten years, from 2006 to 2016, however, regardless of the skill of the observers it cannot be compared to a verified tape measurement, especially considering the uncertainty inherent in visual size estimation in the wild.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bayliss_164-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bayliss-164">[163]</a></sup> In addition, this region may contain up to four other specimens measuring over 6.1 m (20 ft 0 in).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-underwatertimes1_162-1"><a href="#cite_note-underwatertimes1-162">[161]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ohmynews1_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-ohmynews1-165">[164]</a></sup></li>
<li>S. Baker (1874) claimed that in <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">Sri Lanka</a> in the 1800s, specimens measuring 6.7 m (22 ft 0 in) or more were commonplace.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wood_35-11"><a href="#cite_note-Wood-35">[35]</a></sup> However, the largest specimen killed on the island that was considered authentic by Guinness Records was a suspected man-eater killed in the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Province,_Sri_Lanka" title="Eastern Province, Sri Lanka">Eastern Province</a> that measured exactly 6 m (19 ft 8 in) in length.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wood_35-12"><a href="#cite_note-Wood-35">[35]</a></sup></li>
<li>In May 1966 Herb Schweighofer shot an individual along the northeastern coast of Papua New Guinea said to measure 6.32 m (20 ft 9 in) long with a belly girth of 2.74 m (9 ft 0 in).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wood_35-13"><a href="#cite_note-Wood-35">[35]</a></sup></li>
</ul>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Saltwater_crocodile&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
